<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bridges_of_Pittsburgh,_Pennsylvania_(4188335803).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1024" data-file-width="768" decoding="async" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Bridges_of_Pittsburgh%2C_Pennsylvania_%284188335803%29.jpg/170px-Bridges_of_Pittsburgh%2C_Pennsylvania_%284188335803%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Bridges_of_Pittsburgh%2C_Pennsylvania_%284188335803%29.jpg/255px-Bridges_of_Pittsburgh%2C_Pennsylvania_%284188335803%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Bridges_of_Pittsburgh%2C_Pennsylvania_%284188335803%29.jpg/340px-Bridges_of_Pittsburgh%2C_Pennsylvania_%284188335803%29.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bridges_of_Pittsburgh,_Pennsylvania_(4188335803).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Underneath the <a href="/wiki/Fort_Pitt_Bridge" title="Fort Pitt Bridge">Fort Pitt Bridge</a> in <a href="/wiki/Pittsburgh" title="Pittsburgh">Pittsburgh</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania" title="Pennsylvania">Pennsylvania</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Vanha_kivisilta_DSC08273_C.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3000" data-file-width="4000" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Vanha_kivisilta_DSC08273_C.JPG/220px-Vanha_kivisilta_DSC08273_C.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Vanha_kivisilta_DSC08273_C.JPG/330px-Vanha_kivisilta_DSC08273_C.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Vanha_kivisilta_DSC08273_C.JPG/440px-Vanha_kivisilta_DSC08273_C.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Vanha_kivisilta_DSC08273_C.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The old stone-made <a href="/wiki/Arch_bridge" title="Arch bridge">arch bridge</a> over the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kerava_River" title="Kerava River">Kerava River</a> in <a href="/wiki/Kerava" title="Kerava">Kerava</a>, <a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">Finland</a></div></div></div>
<p>A <b>bridge</b> is a structure built to <a href="/wiki/Span_(engineering)" title="Span (engineering)">span</a> a physical obstacle (such as a body of water, valley, road, or rail) without blocking the way underneath. It is constructed for the purpose of providing passage over the obstacle, which is usually something that is otherwise difficult or impossible to cross. There are many different designs of bridges, each serving a particular purpose and applicable to different situations. Designs of bridges vary depending on factors such as the function of the bridge, the nature of the <a href="/wiki/Terrain" title="Terrain">terrain</a> where the bridge is constructed and anchored, and the material used to make it, and the funds available to build it.
</p><p>The earliest bridges were likely made with fallen trees and <a href="/wiki/Stepping_stones" title="Stepping stones">stepping stones</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a> people built <a href="/wiki/Boardwalk" title="Boardwalk">boardwalk</a> bridges across marshland. The <a href="/wiki/Arkadiko_Bridge" title="Arkadiko Bridge">Arkadiko Bridge</a> (dating from the 13th century BC, in the <a href="/wiki/Peloponnese" title="Peloponnese">Peloponnese</a>) is one of the oldest <a href="/wiki/Arch_bridge" title="Arch bridge">arch bridges</a> still in existence and use.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bridge&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Stone_Bridge_(Skopje)_20180223.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3456" data-file-width="5184" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Stone_Bridge_%28Skopje%29_20180223.jpg/220px-Stone_Bridge_%28Skopje%29_20180223.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Stone_Bridge_%28Skopje%29_20180223.jpg/330px-Stone_Bridge_%28Skopje%29_20180223.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Stone_Bridge_%28Skopje%29_20180223.jpg/440px-Stone_Bridge_%28Skopje%29_20180223.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Stone_Bridge_(Skopje)_20180223.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Stone_Bridge_(Skopje)" title="Stone Bridge (Skopje)">Stone Bridge</a> in <a href="/wiki/Skopje" title="Skopje">Skopje</a>, <a href="/wiki/North_Macedonia" title="North Macedonia">North Macedonia</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sio_se_pol.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="311" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="86" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Sio_se_pol.jpg/220px-Sio_se_pol.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Sio_se_pol.jpg/330px-Sio_se_pol.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Sio_se_pol.jpg/440px-Sio_se_pol.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sio_se_pol.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Siosepol" title="Siosepol">Siosepol</a> bridge over <a href="/wiki/Zayanderud" title="Zayanderud">Zayandeh River</a> is an example of <a href="/wiki/Safavid_dynasty" title="Safavid dynasty">Safavid dynasty</a> (1502–1722) bridge design. <a href="/wiki/Isfahan" title="Isfahan">Isfahan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a>.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Aerial_photographs_taken_from_the_Wilderness_car_park,_Wetherby_(4th_May_2021)_006.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2250" data-file-width="4000" decoding="async" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Aerial_photographs_taken_from_the_Wilderness_car_park%2C_Wetherby_%284th_May_2021%29_006.jpg/220px-Aerial_photographs_taken_from_the_Wilderness_car_park%2C_Wetherby_%284th_May_2021%29_006.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Aerial_photographs_taken_from_the_Wilderness_car_park%2C_Wetherby_%284th_May_2021%29_006.jpg/330px-Aerial_photographs_taken_from_the_Wilderness_car_park%2C_Wetherby_%284th_May_2021%29_006.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Aerial_photographs_taken_from_the_Wilderness_car_park%2C_Wetherby_%284th_May_2021%29_006.jpg/440px-Aerial_photographs_taken_from_the_Wilderness_car_park%2C_Wetherby_%284th_May_2021%29_006.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Aerial_photographs_taken_from_the_Wilderness_car_park,_Wetherby_(4th_May_2021)_006.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The 13th century <a href="/wiki/Wetherby_Bridge" title="Wetherby Bridge">Wetherby Bridge</a> spans the <a href="/wiki/River_Wharfe" title="River Wharfe">River Wharfe</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The <i><a href="/wiki/Oxford_English_Dictionary" title="Oxford English Dictionary">Oxford English Dictionary</a></i> traces the origin of the word <i>bridge</i> to an <a href="/wiki/Old_English" title="Old English">Old English</a> word <i>brycg</i>, of the same meaning.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> The word can be traced directly back to Proto-Indo-European <i>*bʰrēw-.</i> The word for the <a href="/wiki/Contract_bridge" title="Contract bridge">card game of the same name</a> has a different origin.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bridge&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Seasonal_bridge_north_of_Jispa,_H.P.,_India._2010.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="230" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" height="49" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Seasonal_bridge_north_of_Jispa%2C_H.P.%2C_India._2010.jpg/220px-Seasonal_bridge_north_of_Jispa%2C_H.P.%2C_India._2010.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Seasonal_bridge_north_of_Jispa%2C_H.P.%2C_India._2010.jpg/330px-Seasonal_bridge_north_of_Jispa%2C_H.P.%2C_India._2010.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Seasonal_bridge_north_of_Jispa%2C_H.P.%2C_India._2010.jpg/440px-Seasonal_bridge_north_of_Jispa%2C_H.P.%2C_India._2010.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Seasonal_bridge_north_of_Jispa,_H.P.,_India._2010.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Seasonal bridge north of <a href="/wiki/Jispa" title="Jispa">Jispa</a>, H.P., India. 2010</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Brug_119_in_de_Lijnbaansgracht_over_de_Bloemgracht_foto_1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3456" data-file-width="4608" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Brug_119_in_de_Lijnbaansgracht_over_de_Bloemgracht_foto_1.jpg/220px-Brug_119_in_de_Lijnbaansgracht_over_de_Bloemgracht_foto_1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Brug_119_in_de_Lijnbaansgracht_over_de_Bloemgracht_foto_1.jpg/330px-Brug_119_in_de_Lijnbaansgracht_over_de_Bloemgracht_foto_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Brug_119_in_de_Lijnbaansgracht_over_de_Bloemgracht_foto_1.jpg/440px-Brug_119_in_de_Lijnbaansgracht_over_de_Bloemgracht_foto_1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Brug_119_in_de_Lijnbaansgracht_over_de_Bloemgracht_foto_1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bridges in <a href="/wiki/Amsterdam" title="Amsterdam">Amsterdam</a>, <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a></div></div></div>
<p>The simplest and earliest types of bridges were <a href="/wiki/Stepping_stones" title="Stepping stones">stepping stones</a>. <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a> people also built a form of <a href="/wiki/Boardwalk" title="Boardwalk">boardwalk</a> across <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marshes" title="Marshes">marshes</a>; examples of such bridges include the <a href="/wiki/Sweet_Track" title="Sweet Track">Sweet Track</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Post_Track" title="Post Track">Post Track</a> in England, approximately 6000 years old.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Current_Archaeology_somerset-levels_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-Current_Archaeology_somerset-levels-2">[2]</a></sup> Undoubtedly, ancient people would also have used <a href="/wiki/Log_bridge" title="Log bridge">log bridges</a>; that is a <a href="/wiki/Timber_bridge" title="Timber bridge">timber bridge</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> that fall naturally or are intentionally felled or placed across streams. Some of the first <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Man-made" title="Man-made">man-made</a> bridges with significant span were probably intentionally felled trees.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p><p>Among the oldest <a href="/wiki/Timber_bridge" title="Timber bridge">timber bridges</a> is the <a href="/wiki/Holzbr%C3%BCcke_Rapperswil-Hurden" title="Holzbrücke Rapperswil-Hurden">Holzbrücke Rapperswil-Hurden</a> crossing upper <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lake_Z%C3%BCrich" title="Lake Zürich">Lake Zürich</a> in Switzerland; the prehistoric timber piles discovered to the west of the <a href="/wiki/Seedamm" title="Seedamm">Seedamm</a> date back to 1523 BC. The first wooden footbridge led across Lake Zürich, followed by several reconstructions at least until the late 2nd century AD, when the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a> built a 6-metre-wide (20 ft) wooden bridge. Between 1358 and 1360, <a href="/wiki/Rudolf_IV,_Duke_of_Austria" title="Rudolf IV, Duke of Austria">Rudolf IV, Duke of Austria</a>, built a 'new' wooden bridge across the lake that has been used to 1878 – measuring approximately 1,450 metres (4,760 ft) in length and 4 metres (13 ft) wide. On April 6, 2001, the reconstructed wooden footbridge was opened, being the longest wooden bridge in Switzerland.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Arkadiko_Bridge" title="Arkadiko Bridge">Arkadiko Bridge</a> is one of four Mycenaean <a href="/wiki/Corbel_arch" title="Corbel arch">corbel arch</a> bridges part of a former network of roads, designed to accommodate <a href="/wiki/Chariot" title="Chariot">chariots</a>, between the fort of Tiryns and town of Epidauros in the <a href="/wiki/Peloponnese" title="Peloponnese">Peloponnese</a>, in southern Greece. Dating to the Greek <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age" title="Bronze Age">Bronze Age</a> (13th century BC), it is one of the oldest <a href="/wiki/Arch_bridge" title="Arch bridge">arch bridges</a> still in existence and use. Several intact arched stone bridges from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hellenistic_age" title="Hellenistic age">Hellenistic era</a> can be found in the Peloponnese.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:303px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Samuel_Beckett_Bridge_At_Sunset_Dublin_Ireland_(97037639)_(cropped).jpeg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="561" data-file-width="1130" decoding="async" height="149" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Samuel_Beckett_Bridge_At_Sunset_Dublin_Ireland_%2897037639%29_%28cropped%29.jpeg/301px-Samuel_Beckett_Bridge_At_Sunset_Dublin_Ireland_%2897037639%29_%28cropped%29.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Samuel_Beckett_Bridge_At_Sunset_Dublin_Ireland_%2897037639%29_%28cropped%29.jpeg/452px-Samuel_Beckett_Bridge_At_Sunset_Dublin_Ireland_%2897037639%29_%28cropped%29.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Samuel_Beckett_Bridge_At_Sunset_Dublin_Ireland_%2897037639%29_%28cropped%29.jpeg/602px-Samuel_Beckett_Bridge_At_Sunset_Dublin_Ireland_%2897037639%29_%28cropped%29.jpeg 2x" width="301"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Samuel_Beckett_Bridge_At_Sunset_Dublin_Ireland_(97037639)_(cropped).jpeg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Samuel_Beckett_Bridge" title="Samuel Beckett Bridge">Samuel Beckett Bridge</a> in <a href="/wiki/Dublin" title="Dublin">Dublin</a>, Ireland</div></div></div>
<p>The greatest bridge builders of antiquity were the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_Engineering" title="Roman Engineering">ancient Romans</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> The Romans built arch bridges and <a href="/wiki/Aqueduct_(bridge)" title="Aqueduct (bridge)">aqueducts</a> that could stand in conditions that would damage or destroy earlier designs. Some stand today.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-historyworld_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-historyworld-7">[7]</a></sup> An example is the <a href="/wiki/Alc%C3%A1ntara_Bridge" title="Alcántara Bridge">Alcántara Bridge</a>, built over the river <a href="/wiki/Tagus" title="Tagus">Tagus</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a>. The Romans also used <a href="/wiki/Cement" title="Cement">cement</a>, which reduced the variation of strength found in natural stone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> One type of cement, called <a href="/wiki/Pozzolana" title="Pozzolana">pozzolana</a>, consisted of water, <a href="/wiki/Lime_(material)" title="Lime (material)">lime</a>, sand, and <a href="/wiki/Volcanic_rock" title="Volcanic rock">volcanic rock</a>. <a href="/wiki/Brick" title="Brick">Brick</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mortar_(masonry)" title="Mortar (masonry)">mortar</a> bridges were built after the Roman era, as the technology for cement was lost (then later rediscovered).
</p><p>In India, the <i><a href="/wiki/Arthashastra" title="Arthashastra">Arthashastra</a></i> treatise by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kautilya" title="Kautilya">Kautilya</a> mentions the construction of dams and bridges.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> A <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mauryan" title="Mauryan">Mauryan</a> bridge near <a href="/wiki/Girnar" title="Girnar">Girnar</a> was surveyed by <a href="/wiki/James_Prinsep" title="James Prinsep">James Princep</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dutt_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dutt-10">[10]</a></sup> The bridge was swept away during a flood, and later repaired by <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Puspagupta&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Puspagupta (page does not exist)">Puspagupta</a>, the chief architect of emperor <a href="/wiki/Chandragupta_I" title="Chandragupta I">Chandragupta I</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dutt_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-Dutt-10">[10]</a></sup> The use of stronger bridges using plaited bamboo and iron chain was visible in India by about the 4th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> A number of bridges, both for military and commercial purposes, were constructed by the <a href="/wiki/Mughal_Empire" title="Mughal Empire">Mughal</a> administration in India.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p><p>Although large Chinese bridges of wooden construction existed at the time of the <a href="/wiki/Warring_States_period" title="Warring States period">Warring States period</a>, the oldest surviving stone bridge in China is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zhaozhou_Bridge" title="Zhaozhou Bridge">Zhaozhou Bridge</a>, built from 595 to 605 AD during the <a href="/wiki/Sui_dynasty" title="Sui dynasty">Sui dynasty</a>. This bridge is also historically significant as it is the world's oldest <a href="/wiki/Spandrel" title="Spandrel">open-spandrel</a> stone segmental arch bridge. European segmental arch bridges date back to at least the <a href="/wiki/Alcon%C3%A9tar_Bridge" title="Alconétar Bridge">Alconétar Bridge</a> (approximately 2nd century AD), while the enormous Roman era <a href="/wiki/Trajan%27s_Bridge" title="Trajan's Bridge">Trajan's Bridge</a> (105 AD) featured open-spandrel segmental arches in wooden construction.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Inca_rope_bridge" title="Inca rope bridge">Rope bridges</a>, a simple type of <a href="/wiki/Suspension_bridge" title="Suspension bridge">suspension bridge</a>, were used by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inca" title="Inca">Inca</a> civilization in the <a href="/wiki/Andes" title="Andes">Andes</a> mountains of South America, just prior to European colonization in the 16th century.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Ashanti_Empire" title="Ashanti Empire">Ashanti</a> built bridges over <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Streams" title="Streams">streams</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rivers" title="Rivers">rivers</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Edgerton_page_38_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Edgerton_page_38-14">[14]</a></sup> They were constructed by pounding four large forked tree trunks into the stream bed, placing beams along these forked pillars, then positioning cross-beams that were finally covered with four to six inches of dirt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Edgerton_page_38_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-Edgerton_page_38-14">[14]</a></sup>
</p><p>During the 18th century, there were many innovations in the design of timber bridges by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hans_Ulrich_Grubenmann" title="Hans Ulrich Grubenmann">Hans Ulrich Grubenmann</a>, <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Grubenmann" title="Johannes Grubenmann">Johannes Grubenmann</a>, and others. The first book on bridge engineering was written by <a href="/wiki/Hubert_Gautier" title="Hubert Gautier">Hubert Gautier</a> in 1716.
</p><p>A major breakthrough in bridge technology came with the erection of <a href="/wiki/The_Iron_Bridge" title="The Iron Bridge">the Iron Bridge</a> in Shropshire, England in 1779. It used <a href="/wiki/Cast_iron" title="Cast iron">cast iron</a> for the first time as arches to cross the <a href="/wiki/River_Severn" title="River Severn">river Severn</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> With the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">Industrial Revolution</a> in the 19th century, <a href="/wiki/Truss" title="Truss">truss</a> systems of <a href="/wiki/Wrought_iron" title="Wrought iron">wrought iron</a> were developed for larger bridges, but iron does not have the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tensile_strength" title="Tensile strength">tensile strength</a> to support large loads. With the advent of steel, which has a high tensile strength, much larger bridges were built, many using the ideas of <a href="/wiki/Gustave_Eiffel" title="Gustave Eiffel">Gustave Eiffel</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:West_Montrose_Covered_Bridge_(Oct._2018).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1701" data-file-width="3600" decoding="async" height="104" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/West_Montrose_Covered_Bridge_%28Oct._2018%29.jpg/220px-West_Montrose_Covered_Bridge_%28Oct._2018%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/West_Montrose_Covered_Bridge_%28Oct._2018%29.jpg/330px-West_Montrose_Covered_Bridge_%28Oct._2018%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/West_Montrose_Covered_Bridge_%28Oct._2018%29.jpg/440px-West_Montrose_Covered_Bridge_%28Oct._2018%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:West_Montrose_Covered_Bridge_(Oct._2018).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The covered bridge in <a href="/wiki/West_Montrose,_Ontario" title="West Montrose, Ontario">West Montrose, Ontario</a>, Canada</div></div></div>
<p>In Canada and the United States, numerous timber <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Covered_bridges" title="Covered bridges">covered bridges</a> were built in the late 1700s to the late 1800s, reminiscent of earlier designs in <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a> and <a href="/wiki/Switzerland" title="Switzerland">Switzerland</a>. Some covered bridges were also built in Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> In later years, some were partly made of stone or metal but the trusses were usually still made of wood; in the United States, there were three styles of trusses, the Queen Post, the Burr Arch and the Town Lattice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> Hundreds of these structures still stand in North America. They were brought to the attention of the general public in the 1990s by the novel, movie, and play <i><a href="/wiki/The_Bridges_of_Madison_County" title="The Bridges of Madison County">The Bridges of Madison County</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1927 <a href="/wiki/Welding" title="Welding">welding</a> pioneer <a href="/wiki/Stefan_Bry%C5%82a" title="Stefan Bryła">Stefan Bryła</a> designed the first welded <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Road_bridge" title="Road bridge">road bridge</a> in the world, the <a href="/wiki/Maurzyce_Bridge" title="Maurzyce Bridge">Maurzyce Bridge</a> which was later built across the river <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/S%C5%82udwia_River" title="Słudwia River">Słudwia</a> at Maurzyce near <a href="/wiki/%C5%81owicz" title="Łowicz">Łowicz</a>, Poland in 1929. In 1995, the <a href="/wiki/American_Welding_Society" title="American Welding Society">American Welding Society</a> presented the Historic Welded Structure Award for the bridge to Poland.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Types_of_bridges">Types of bridges</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bridge&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Types of bridges">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Bridges can be categorized in several different ways. Common categories include the type of structural elements used, by what they carry, whether they are fixed or movable, and by the materials used.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Structure_types">Structure types</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bridge&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Structure types">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Bridges may be classified by how the actions of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tension_(mechanics)" title="Tension (mechanics)">tension</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Compression_(physical)" title="Compression (physical)">compression</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bending" title="Bending">bending</a>, <a href="/wiki/Torsion_(mechanics)" title="Torsion (mechanics)">torsion</a> and <a href="/wiki/Shear_stress" title="Shear stress">shear</a> are distributed through their structure. Most bridges will employ all of these to some degree, but only a few will predominate. The separation of forces and moments may be quite clear. In a suspension or cable-stayed bridge, the elements in tension are distinct in shape and placement. In other cases the forces may be distributed among a large number of members, as in a truss.
</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<table class="wikitable">
<tbody><tr>
<td style="text-align:center;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:BeamBridge-diagram.svg"><img alt="BeamBridge-diagram.svg" data-file-height="654" data-file-width="1048" decoding="async" height="125" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/BeamBridge-diagram.svg/200px-BeamBridge-diagram.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/BeamBridge-diagram.svg/300px-BeamBridge-diagram.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/BeamBridge-diagram.svg/400px-BeamBridge-diagram.svg.png 2x" width="200"/></a><b>Beam bridge</b><div style="clear:both;"></div>
</td>
<td><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Beam_bridges" title="Beam bridges">Beam bridges</a> are horizontal beams supported at each end by substructure units and can be either <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Simply_supported" title="Simply supported">simply supported</a></i> when the beams only connect across a single span, or <i>continuous</i> when the beams are connected across two or more spans. When there are multiple spans, the intermediate supports are known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bridge_pier" title="Bridge pier">piers</a>. The earliest beam bridges were simple logs that sat across streams and similar simple structures. In modern times, beam bridges can range from small, wooden beams to large, steel boxes. The vertical force on the bridge becomes a <a href="/wiki/Shear_stress" title="Shear stress">shear</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bending" title="Bending">flexural</a> load on the beam which is transferred down its length to the substructures on either side<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-beambridge_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-beambridge-22">[22]</a></sup> They are typically made of steel, concrete or wood. <a href="/wiki/Girder_bridge" title="Girder bridge">Girder bridges</a> and <a href="/wiki/Plate_girder_bridge" title="Plate girder bridge">plate girder bridges</a>, usually made from steel, are types of beam bridges. <a href="/wiki/Box_girder_bridge" title="Box girder bridge">Box girder bridges</a>, made from steel, concrete, or both, are also beam bridges. Beam bridge spans rarely exceed 250 feet (76 m) long, as the flexural stresses increase proportionally to the square of the length (and deflection increases proportionally to the 4th power of the length).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> However, the main span of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rio%E2%80%93Niteroi_Bridge" title="Rio–Niteroi Bridge">Rio–Niteroi Bridge</a>, a box girder bridge, is 300 metres (980 ft).<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
<p>The world's longest beam bridge is <a href="/wiki/Lake_Pontchartrain_Causeway" title="Lake Pontchartrain Causeway">Lake Pontchartrain Causeway</a> in southern <a href="/wiki/Louisiana" title="Louisiana">Louisiana</a> in the United States, at 23.83 miles (38.35 km), with individual spans of 56 feet (17 m).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> Beam bridges are the simplest and oldest type of bridge in use today,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> and are a popular type.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup>
</p>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td style="text-align:center;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:TrussBridge-diagram.svg"><img alt="TrussBridge-diagram.svg" data-file-height="654" data-file-width="1048" decoding="async" height="125" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/TrussBridge-diagram.svg/200px-TrussBridge-diagram.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/TrussBridge-diagram.svg/300px-TrussBridge-diagram.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/TrussBridge-diagram.svg/400px-TrussBridge-diagram.svg.png 2x" width="200"/></a><b>Truss bridge</b><div style="clear:both;"></div>
</td>
<td>A <a href="/wiki/Truss_bridge" title="Truss bridge">truss bridge</a> is a bridge whose load-bearing superstructure is composed of a truss. This truss is a structure of connected elements forming triangular units. The connected elements (typically straight) may be stressed from tension, compression, or sometimes both in response to dynamic loads. Truss bridges are one of the oldest types of modern bridges. The basic types of truss bridges shown in this article have simple designs which could be easily analyzed by nineteenth and early twentieth-century engineers. A truss bridge is economical to construct owing to its efficient use of materials.
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td style="text-align:center;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:CantileverBridge-diagram.svg"><img alt="CantileverBridge-diagram.svg" data-file-height="654" data-file-width="1050" decoding="async" height="125" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/CantileverBridge-diagram.svg/200px-CantileverBridge-diagram.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/CantileverBridge-diagram.svg/300px-CantileverBridge-diagram.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/CantileverBridge-diagram.svg/400px-CantileverBridge-diagram.svg.png 2x" width="200"/></a><b>Cantilever bridge</b><div style="clear:both;"></div>
</td>
<td><a href="/wiki/Cantilever_bridge" title="Cantilever bridge">Cantilever bridges</a> are built using <a href="/wiki/Cantilever" title="Cantilever">cantilevers</a>—horizontal beams supported on only one end. Most cantilever bridges use a pair of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Continuous_span" title="Continuous span">continuous spans</a> that extend from opposite sides of the supporting piers to meet at the center of the obstacle the bridge crosses. Cantilever bridges are constructed using much the same materials and techniques as beam bridges. The difference comes in the action of the forces through the bridge.
<p>Some cantilever bridges also have a smaller beam connecting the two cantilevers, for extra strength.
</p><p>The largest cantilever bridge is the 549-metre (1,801 ft) <a href="/wiki/Quebec_Bridge" title="Quebec Bridge">Quebec Bridge</a> in Quebec, Canada.
</p>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td style="text-align:center;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ArchBridge-diagram.svg"><img alt="ArchBridge-diagram.svg" data-file-height="654" data-file-width="1048" decoding="async" height="125" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/ArchBridge-diagram.svg/200px-ArchBridge-diagram.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/ArchBridge-diagram.svg/300px-ArchBridge-diagram.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/ArchBridge-diagram.svg/400px-ArchBridge-diagram.svg.png 2x" width="200"/></a><b>Arch bridge</b><div style="clear:both;"></div>
</td>
<td><a href="/wiki/Arch_bridge" title="Arch bridge">Arch bridges</a> have <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abutments" title="Abutments">abutments</a> at each end. The weight of the bridge is thrust into the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abutments" title="Abutments">abutments</a> at either side. The earliest known arch bridges were built by the Greeks, and include the <a href="/wiki/Arkadiko_Bridge" title="Arkadiko Bridge">Arkadiko Bridge</a>.
<p>With the span of 220 metres (720 ft), the <a href="/wiki/Solkan_Bridge" title="Solkan Bridge">Solkan Bridge</a> over the <a href="/wiki/So%C4%8Da" title="Soča">Soča</a> River at <a href="/wiki/Solkan" title="Solkan">Solkan</a> in Slovenia is the second-largest stone bridge in the world and the longest railroad stone bridge. It was completed in 1905. Its arch, which was constructed from over 5,000 tonnes (4,900 long tons; 5,500 short tons) of stone blocks in just 18 days, is the second-largest stone arch in the world, surpassed only by the Friedensbrücke (Syratalviadukt) in <a href="/wiki/Plauen" title="Plauen">Plauen</a>, and the largest railroad stone arch. The arch of the Friedensbrücke, which was built in the same year, has the span of 90 m (295 ft) and crosses the valley of the <a href="/wiki/Syrabach" title="Syrabach">Syrabach</a> River. The difference between the two is that the Solkan Bridge was built from stone blocks, whereas the Friedensbrücke was built from a mixture of crushed stone and cement mortar.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup>
</p><p>The world's largest arch bridge is the <a href="/wiki/Chaotianmen_Bridge" title="Chaotianmen Bridge">Chaotianmen Bridge</a> over the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yangtze_River" title="Yangtze River">Yangtze River</a> with a length of 1,741 m (5,712 ft) and a span of 552 m (1,811 ft). The bridge was opened April 29, 2009, in <a href="/wiki/Chongqing" title="Chongqing">Chongqing</a>, China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chaotianmen_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chaotianmen-28">[28]</a></sup>
</p>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td style="text-align:center;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:TiedarchBridge-diagram.svg"><img alt="TiedarchBridge-diagram.svg" data-file-height="654" data-file-width="1048" decoding="async" height="125" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/TiedarchBridge-diagram.svg/200px-TiedarchBridge-diagram.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/TiedarchBridge-diagram.svg/300px-TiedarchBridge-diagram.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/TiedarchBridge-diagram.svg/400px-TiedarchBridge-diagram.svg.png 2x" width="200"/></a><b>Tied arch bridge</b><div style="clear:both;"></div>
</td>
<td><a href="/wiki/Tied-arch_bridge" title="Tied-arch bridge">Tied-arch bridges</a> have an arch-shaped superstructure, but differ from conventional arch bridges. Instead of transferring the weight of the bridge and traffic loads into thrust forces into the abutments, the ends of the arches are restrained by tension in the bottom chord of the structure. They are also called bowstring arches.
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td style="text-align:center;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:SuspensionBridge-diagram.svg"><img alt="SuspensionBridge-diagram.svg" data-file-height="654" data-file-width="1048" decoding="async" height="125" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/SuspensionBridge-diagram.svg/200px-SuspensionBridge-diagram.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/SuspensionBridge-diagram.svg/300px-SuspensionBridge-diagram.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/SuspensionBridge-diagram.svg/400px-SuspensionBridge-diagram.svg.png 2x" width="200"/></a><b>Suspension bridge</b><div style="clear:both;"></div>
</td>
<td>Suspension bridges are suspended from cables. The earliest suspension bridges were made of ropes or vines covered with pieces of bamboo. In modern bridges, the cables hang from towers that are attached to caissons or cofferdams. The caissons or cofferdams are implanted deep into the bed of the lake, river or sea. Sub-types include the <a href="/wiki/Simple_suspension_bridge" title="Simple suspension bridge">simple suspension bridge</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Stressed_ribbon_bridge" title="Stressed ribbon bridge">stressed ribbon bridge</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Underspanned_suspension_bridge" title="Underspanned suspension bridge">underspanned suspension bridge</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Suspended-deck_suspension_bridge" title="Suspended-deck suspension bridge">suspended-deck suspension bridge</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Self-anchored_suspension_bridge" title="Self-anchored suspension bridge">self-anchored suspension bridge</a>. There is also what is sometimes called a "semi-suspension" bridge, of which the <a href="/wiki/Ferry_Bridge,_Burton" title="Ferry Bridge, Burton">Ferry Bridge</a> in Burton-upon-Trent is the only one of its kind in Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup>
<p>The longest suspension bridge in the world is the 4,608 m (15,118 ft) <a href="/wiki/1915_%C3%87anakkale_Bridge" title="1915 Çanakkale Bridge">1915 Çanakkale Bridge</a> in Turkey.
</p>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td style="text-align:center;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:CableStayedBridge-diagram.svg"><img alt="CableStayedBridge-diagram.svg" data-file-height="654" data-file-width="1048" decoding="async" height="125" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/CableStayedBridge-diagram.svg/200px-CableStayedBridge-diagram.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/CableStayedBridge-diagram.svg/300px-CableStayedBridge-diagram.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/CableStayedBridge-diagram.svg/400px-CableStayedBridge-diagram.svg.png 2x" width="200"/></a><b>Cable-stayed bridge</b><div style="clear:both;"></div>
</td>
<td><a href="/wiki/Cable-stayed_bridge" title="Cable-stayed bridge">Cable-stayed bridges</a>, like suspension bridges, are held up by cables. However, in a cable-stayed bridge, less cable is required and the towers holding the cables are proportionately higher.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cable_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-cable-30">[30]</a></sup> The first known cable-stayed bridge was designed in 1784 by C. T. (or C. J.) Löscher.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup>
<p>The longest cable-stayed bridge since 2012 is the 1,104 m (3,622 ft) <a href="/wiki/Russky_Bridge" title="Russky Bridge">Russky Bridge</a> in <a href="/wiki/Vladivostok" title="Vladivostok">Vladivostok</a>, <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup>
</p>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Some Engineers sub-divide 'beam' bridges into slab, beam-and-slab and box girder on the basis of their cross-section.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-34">[34]</a></sup> A slab can be solid or <a href="/wiki/Hollow-core_slab" title="Hollow-core slab">voided</a> (though this is no longer favored for inspectability reasons) while beam-and-slab consists of concrete or steel girders connected by a concrete slab.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-:2-35">[35]</a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/Box_girder_bridge" title="Box girder bridge">box-girder</a> cross-section consists of a single-cell or multi-cellular box. In recent years, <a href="/wiki/Integral_bridge" title="Integral bridge">integral bridge</a> construction has also become popular.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fixed_or_movable_bridges">Fixed or movable bridges</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bridge&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Fixed or movable bridges">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
