codecs="vp8, vorbis"'/></video> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Moving_a_Bloomingdale_Trail_bridge_from_Ashland_to_Western_on_a_Saturday_in_Chicago.webmhd.webm" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Moving a <a href="/wiki/Bloomingdale_Trail" title="Bloomingdale Trail">Bloomingdale Trail</a> bridge from Ashland to Western in <a href="/wiki/Chicago" title="Chicago">Chicago</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Most bridges are fixed bridges, meaning they have no moving parts and stay in one place until they fail or are demolished. Temporary bridges, such as <a href="/wiki/Bailey_bridge" title="Bailey bridge">Bailey bridges</a>, are designed to be assembled, taken apart, transported to a different site, and re-used. They are important in military engineering and are also used to carry traffic while an old bridge is being rebuilt. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Movable_bridge" title="Movable bridge">Movable bridges</a> are designed to move out of the way of boats or other kinds of traffic, which would otherwise be too tall to fit. These are generally electrically powered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:AVLB_01.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2048" data-file-width="3072" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/AVLB_01.jpg/220px-AVLB_01.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/AVLB_01.jpg/330px-AVLB_01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/AVLB_01.jpg/440px-AVLB_01.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:AVLB_01.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Tank bridge transporter of the United States Army. These are mobile bridges; tanks and other vehicles can use them to cross certain obstacles.</div></div></div>
<p>The Tank bridge transporter (TBT) has the same cross-country performance as a tank even when fully loaded. It can deploy, drop off and load bridges independently, but it cannot recover them.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Double-decked_bridges">Double-decked bridges<span class="anchor" id="Double-decked"></span></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bridge&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Double-decked bridges">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:George_Washington_Bridge_from_New_Jersey-edit.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="727" data-file-width="738" decoding="async" height="167" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/George_Washington_Bridge_from_New_Jersey-edit.jpg/170px-George_Washington_Bridge_from_New_Jersey-edit.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/George_Washington_Bridge_from_New_Jersey-edit.jpg/255px-George_Washington_Bridge_from_New_Jersey-edit.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/George_Washington_Bridge_from_New_Jersey-edit.jpg/340px-George_Washington_Bridge_from_New_Jersey-edit.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:George_Washington_Bridge_from_New_Jersey-edit.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The double-decked <a href="/wiki/George_Washington_Bridge" title="George Washington Bridge">George Washington Bridge</a>, connecting <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a> to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bergen_County" title="Bergen County">Bergen County</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_Jersey" title="New Jersey">New Jersey</a>, US, is the world's busiest bridge, carrying 102 million vehicles annually.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-panynj.gov_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-panynj.gov-37">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-abcgwb_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-abcgwb-38">[38]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_multi-level_bridges" title="List of multi-level bridges">List of multi-level bridges</a></div>
<p>Double-decked (or double-decker) bridges have two levels, such as the <a href="/wiki/George_Washington_Bridge" title="George Washington Bridge">George Washington Bridge</a>, connecting <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a> to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bergen_County" title="Bergen County">Bergen County</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_Jersey" title="New Jersey">New Jersey</a>, US, as the world's busiest bridge, carrying 102 million vehicles annually;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-panynj.gov_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-panynj.gov-37">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-abcgwb_38-1"><a href="#cite_note-abcgwb-38">[38]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Truss" title="Truss">truss</a> work between the roadway levels provided stiffness to the roadways and reduced movement of the upper level when the lower level was installed three decades after the upper level. The <a href="/wiki/Tsing_Ma_Bridge" title="Tsing Ma Bridge">Tsing Ma Bridge</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kap_Shui_Mun_Bridge" title="Kap Shui Mun Bridge">Kap Shui Mun Bridge</a> in <a href="/wiki/Hong_Kong" title="Hong Kong">Hong Kong</a> have six lanes on their upper decks, and on their lower decks there are two lanes and a pair of tracks for <a href="/wiki/MTR" title="MTR">MTR</a> metro trains. Some double-decked bridges only use one level for street traffic; the <a href="/wiki/Washington_Avenue_Bridge_(Minneapolis)" title="Washington Avenue Bridge (Minneapolis)">Washington Avenue Bridge</a> in <a href="/wiki/Minneapolis" title="Minneapolis">Minneapolis</a> reserves its lower level for automobile and light rail traffic and its upper level for pedestrian and bicycle traffic (predominantly students at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Minnesota" title="University of Minnesota">University of Minnesota</a>). Likewise, in <a href="/wiki/Toronto" title="Toronto">Toronto</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Prince_Edward_Viaduct" title="Prince Edward Viaduct">Prince Edward Viaduct</a> has five lanes of motor traffic, bicycle lanes, and sidewalks on its upper deck; and a pair of tracks for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bloor%E2%80%93Danforth_line" title="Bloor–Danforth line">Bloor–Danforth</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Toronto_subway_and_RT" title="Toronto subway and RT">subway line</a> on its lower deck. The western span of the <a href="/wiki/San_Francisco%E2%80%93Oakland_Bay_Bridge" title="San Francisco–Oakland Bay Bridge">San Francisco–Oakland Bay Bridge</a> also has two levels.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Robert_Stephenson" title="Robert Stephenson">Robert Stephenson</a>'s <a href="/wiki/High_Level_Bridge,_River_Tyne" title="High Level Bridge, River Tyne">High Level Bridge</a> across the <a href="/wiki/River_Tyne" title="River Tyne">River Tyne</a> in <a href="/wiki/Newcastle_upon_Tyne" title="Newcastle upon Tyne">Newcastle upon Tyne</a>, completed in 1849, is an early example of a double-decked bridge. The upper level carries a railway, and the lower level is used for road traffic. Other examples include <a href="/wiki/Britannia_Bridge" title="Britannia Bridge">Britannia Bridge</a> over the <a href="/wiki/Menai_Strait" title="Menai Strait">Menai Strait</a> and <a href="/wiki/Craigavon_Bridge" title="Craigavon Bridge">Craigavon Bridge</a> in <a href="/wiki/Derry" title="Derry">Derry</a>, <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland" title="Northern Ireland">Northern Ireland</a>. The <a href="/wiki/%C3%98resund_Bridge" title="Øresund Bridge">Oresund Bridge</a> between <a href="/wiki/Copenhagen" title="Copenhagen">Copenhagen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Malm%C3%B6" title="Malmö">Malmö</a> consists of a four-lane highway on the upper level and a pair of railway tracks at the lower level. <a href="/wiki/Tower_Bridge" title="Tower Bridge">Tower Bridge</a> in London is different example of a double-decked bridge, with the central section consisting of a low-level <a href="/wiki/Bascule_bridge" title="Bascule bridge">bascule span</a> and a high-level <a href="/wiki/Footbridge" title="Footbridge">footbridge</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Viaducts">Viaducts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bridge&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Viaducts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Viaduct" title="Viaduct">Viaduct</a></div>
<p>A viaduct is made up of multiple bridges connected into one longer structure. The longest and some of the highest bridges are viaducts, such as the <a href="/wiki/Lake_Pontchartrain_Causeway" title="Lake Pontchartrain Causeway">Lake Pontchartrain Causeway</a> and <a href="/wiki/Millau_Viaduct" title="Millau Viaduct">Millau Viaduct</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Multi-way_bridge">Multi-way bridge</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bridge&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Multi-way bridge">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tridge_Undercarriage.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1949" data-file-width="2974" decoding="async" height="144" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Tridge_Undercarriage.jpg/220px-Tridge_Undercarriage.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Tridge_Undercarriage.jpg/330px-Tridge_Undercarriage.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Tridge_Undercarriage.jpg/440px-Tridge_Undercarriage.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tridge_Undercarriage.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/The_Tridge_(Midland,_Michigan)" title="The Tridge (Midland, Michigan)">Tridge</a> is a type of <a href="/wiki/Multi-way_bridge" title="Multi-way bridge">multi-way bridge</a></div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Multi-way_bridge" title="Multi-way bridge">Multi-way bridge</a></div>
<p>A multi-way bridge has three or more separate spans which meet near the center of the bridge. Multi-way bridges with only three spans appear as a "T" or "Y" when viewed from above. Multi-way bridges are extremely rare. <a href="/wiki/The_Tridge_(Midland,_Michigan)" title="The Tridge (Midland, Michigan)">The Tridge</a>, <a href="/wiki/Margaret_Bridge" title="Margaret Bridge">Margaret Bridge</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Y-Bridge_(Zanesville,_Ohio)" title="Y-Bridge (Zanesville, Ohio)">Zanesville Y-Bridge</a> are examples.
</p><p><span class="anchor" id="road_bridge"></span>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bridge_types_by_use">Bridge types by use</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bridge&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Bridge types by use">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A bridge can be categorized by what it is designed to carry, such as trains, pedestrian or road traffic (<b>road bridge</b>), a pipeline or waterway for water transport or barge traffic. An <a href="/wiki/Aqueduct_(bridge)" title="Aqueduct (bridge)">aqueduct</a> is a bridge that carries water, resembling a viaduct, which is a bridge that connects points of equal height. A road-rail bridge carries both road and rail traffic. Overway is a term for a bridge that separates incompatible intersecting traffic, especially road and rail.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> A bridge can carry overhead power lines as does the <a href="/wiki/Storstr%C3%B8m_Bridge" title="Storstrøm Bridge">Storstrøm Bridge</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Some bridges accommodate other purposes, such as the tower of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nov%C3%BD_Most" title="Nový Most">Nový Most</a> Bridge in <a href="/wiki/Bratislava" title="Bratislava">Bratislava</a>, which features a restaurant, or a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bridge-restaurant" title="Bridge-restaurant">bridge-restaurant</a> which is a bridge built to serve as a restaurant. Other suspension bridge towers carry transmission antennas.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Conservationists use <a href="/wiki/Wildlife_crossing" title="Wildlife crossing">wildlife overpasses</a> to reduce <a href="/wiki/Habitat_fragmentation" title="Habitat fragmentation">habitat fragmentation</a> and animal-vehicle collisions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> The first <a href="/wiki/Wildlife_crossing" title="Wildlife crossing">animal bridges</a> sprung up in France in the 1950s, and these types of bridges are now used worldwide to protect both large and small wildlife.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup>
</p><p>Bridges are subject to unplanned uses as well. The areas underneath some bridges have become makeshift shelters and homes to homeless people, and the undertimbers of bridges all around the world are spots of prevalent graffiti. Some bridges attract people attempting <a href="/wiki/Suicide" title="Suicide">suicide</a>, and become known as <a href="/wiki/Suicide_bridge" title="Suicide bridge">suicide bridges</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bridge_types_by_material">Bridge types by material</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bridge&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Bridge types by material">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ironbridge_6.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1648" data-file-width="2216" decoding="async" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Ironbridge_6.jpg/220px-Ironbridge_6.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Ironbridge_6.jpg/330px-Ironbridge_6.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Ironbridge_6.jpg/440px-Ironbridge_6.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ironbridge_6.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/The_Iron_Bridge" title="The Iron Bridge">The Iron Bridge</a> completed in 1781 was the first cast iron bridge.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kraemerbruecke_und_Aegidienkirche_Erfurt_2017.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3953" data-file-width="5861" decoding="async" height="148" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Kraemerbruecke_und_Aegidienkirche_Erfurt_2017.jpg/220px-Kraemerbruecke_und_Aegidienkirche_Erfurt_2017.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Kraemerbruecke_und_Aegidienkirche_Erfurt_2017.jpg/330px-Kraemerbruecke_und_Aegidienkirche_Erfurt_2017.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Kraemerbruecke_und_Aegidienkirche_Erfurt_2017.jpg/440px-Kraemerbruecke_und_Aegidienkirche_Erfurt_2017.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kraemerbruecke_und_Aegidienkirche_Erfurt_2017.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Kr%C3%A4merbr%C3%BCcke" title="Krämerbrücke">Krämerbrücke</a> in <a href="/wiki/Erfurt" title="Erfurt">Erfurt</a>, Germany – with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Half_timbered" title="Half timbered">half timbered</a> buildings</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Stone_bridge,_Othoni_island.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2448" data-file-width="3264" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Stone_bridge%2C_Othoni_island.jpg/220px-Stone_bridge%2C_Othoni_island.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Stone_bridge%2C_Othoni_island.jpg/330px-Stone_bridge%2C_Othoni_island.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Stone_bridge%2C_Othoni_island.jpg/440px-Stone_bridge%2C_Othoni_island.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Stone_bridge,_Othoni_island.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Small stone bridge, <a href="/wiki/Othonoi" title="Othonoi">Othonoi</a>, Greece</div></div></div>
<p>The materials used to build the structure are also used to categorize bridges. Until the end of the 18th century, bridges were made out of timber, stone and masonry. Modern bridges are currently built in concrete, steel, fiber reinforced polymers (FRP), stainless steel or combinations of those materials. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Living_root_bridges" title="Living root bridges">Living bridges</a> have been constructed of live plants such as <i><a href="/wiki/Ficus_elastica" title="Ficus elastica">Ficus elastica</a></i> tree roots in India<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Wisteria" title="Wisteria">wisteria</a> vines in Japan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup>
</p>
<table class="wikitable">
<tbody><tr>
<th>Bridge type</th>
<th>Materials used
</th></tr>
<tr>
<td>Cantilever</td>
<td>For small footbridges, the cantilevers may be simple beams; however, large cantilever bridges designed to handle road or rail traffic use trusses built from <a href="/wiki/Structural_steel" title="Structural steel">structural steel</a>, or box girders built from <a href="/wiki/Prestressed_concrete" title="Prestressed concrete">prestressed concrete</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>Suspension</td>
<td>The cables are usually made of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Steel_cable" title="Steel cable">steel cables</a> galvanised with <a href="/wiki/Zinc" title="Zinc">zinc</a>,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="Galvanising is rare on highly stressed structural wires as it can cause cracking. (September 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> along with most of the bridge, but some bridges are still made with steel-<a href="/wiki/Reinforced_concrete" title="Reinforced concrete">reinforced concrete</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>Arch</td>
<td><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stone" title="Stone">Stone</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brick" title="Brick">brick</a> and other such materials that are strong in compression and somewhat so in shear.
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>Beam</td>
<td>Beam bridges can use pre-stressed concrete, an inexpensive building material, which is then embedded with <a href="/wiki/Rebar" title="Rebar">rebar</a>. The resulting bridge can resist both compression and tension forces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>Truss</td>
<td>The triangular pieces of truss bridges are manufactured from straight and steel bars, according to the truss bridge designs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Analysis_and_design">Analysis and design</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bridge&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Analysis and design">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Burbank_Ave_I-5_overpass_under_construction_in_Burbank,_California.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2500" data-file-width="5000" decoding="async" height="110" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Burbank_Ave_I-5_overpass_under_construction_in_Burbank%2C_California.JPG/220px-Burbank_Ave_I-5_overpass_under_construction_in_Burbank%2C_California.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Burbank_Ave_I-5_overpass_under_construction_in_Burbank%2C_California.JPG/330px-Burbank_Ave_I-5_overpass_under_construction_in_Burbank%2C_California.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Burbank_Ave_I-5_overpass_under_construction_in_Burbank%2C_California.JPG/440px-Burbank_Ave_I-5_overpass_under_construction_in_Burbank%2C_California.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Burbank_Ave_I-5_overpass_under_construction_in_Burbank,_California.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Highway <a href="/wiki/Overpass" title="Overpass">overpass</a> under construction in 2021, over <a href="/wiki/Interstate_5_in_California" title="Interstate 5 in California">Interstate 5</a> in <a href="/wiki/Burbank,_California" title="Burbank, California">Burbank, California</a></div></div></div>
<p>Unlike buildings whose design is led by architects, bridges are usually designed by engineers. This follows from the importance of the engineering requirements; namely spanning the obstacle and having the durability to survive, with minimal maintenance, in an aggressive outdoor environment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-:2-35">[35]</a></sup> Bridges are first analysed; the bending moment and shear force distributions are calculated due to the applied loads. For this, the <a href="/wiki/Finite_element_method" title="Finite element method">finite element method</a> is the most popular. The analysis can be one-, two-, or three-dimensional. For the majority of bridges, a two-dimensional plate model (often with stiffening beams) is sufficient or an upstand finite element model.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> On completion of the analysis, the bridge is designed to resist the applied bending moments and shear forces, section sizes are selected with sufficient capacity to resist the stresses. Many bridges are made of <a href="/wiki/Prestressed_concrete" title="Prestressed concrete">prestressed concrete</a> which has good durability properties, either by pre-tensioning of beams prior to installation or post-tensioning on site.
</p><p>In most countries, bridges, like other structures, are designed according to <a href="/wiki/Limit_state_design" title="Limit state design">Load and Resistance Factor Design</a> (LRFD) principles. In simple terms, this means that the load is factored up by a factor greater than unity, while the resistance or capacity of the structure is factored down, by a factor less than unity. The effect of the factored load (stress, bending moment) should be less than the factored resistance to that effect. Both of these factors allow for uncertainty and are greater when the uncertainty is greater.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Aesthetics">Aesthetics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bridge&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Aesthetics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Utrecht_%27Prins_Claus_brug%27.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="684" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Utrecht_%27Prins_Claus_brug%27.jpg/220px-Utrecht_%27Prins_Claus_brug%27.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Utrecht_%27Prins_Claus_brug%27.jpg/330px-Utrecht_%27Prins_Claus_brug%27.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Utrecht_%27Prins_Claus_brug%27.jpg/440px-Utrecht_%27Prins_Claus_brug%27.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Utrecht_%27Prins_Claus_brug%27.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Prins Clausbrug across the <a href="/wiki/Amsterdam%E2%80%93Rhine_Canal" title="Amsterdam–Rhine Canal">Amsterdam–Rhine Canal</a> in <a href="/wiki/Utrecht" title="Utrecht">Utrecht</a>, <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Stari_Most22.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1600" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Stari_Most22.jpg/220px-Stari_Most22.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Stari_Most22.jpg/330px-Stari_Most22.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Stari_Most22.jpg/440px-Stari_Most22.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Stari_Most22.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The World Heritage Site of <a href="/wiki/Stari_Most" title="Stari Most">Stari Most</a> (Old Bridge) gives its name to the city of <a href="/wiki/Mostar" title="Mostar">Mostar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bosnia_and_Herzegovina" title="Bosnia and Herzegovina">Bosnia and Herzegovina</a></div></div></div>
<p>Most bridges are utilitarian in appearance, but in some cases, the appearance of the bridge can have great importance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> Often, this is the case with a large bridge that serves as an entrance to a city, or crosses over a main harbor entrance. These are sometimes known as signature bridges. Designers of bridges in parks and along parkways often place more importance on aesthetics, as well. Examples include the stone-faced bridges along the <a href="/wiki/Taconic_State_Parkway" title="Taconic State Parkway">Taconic State Parkway</a> in New York.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bridge_to_Pier_6,_Gatwick_North_Terminal_-_geograph.org.uk_-_74055.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="478" data-file-width="640" decoding="async" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Bridge_to_Pier_6%2C_Gatwick_North_Terminal_-_geograph.org.uk_-_74055.jpg/220px-Bridge_to_Pier_6%2C_Gatwick_North_Terminal_-_geograph.org.uk_-_74055.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Bridge_to_Pier_6%2C_Gatwick_North_Terminal_-_geograph.org.uk_-_74055.jpg/330px-Bridge_to_Pier_6%2C_Gatwick_North_Terminal_-_geograph.org.uk_-_74055.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Bridge_to_Pier_6%2C_Gatwick_North_Terminal_-_geograph.org.uk_-_74055.jpg/440px-Bridge_to_Pier_6%2C_Gatwick_North_Terminal_-_geograph.org.uk_-_74055.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bridge_to_Pier_6,_Gatwick_North_Terminal_-_geograph.org.uk_-_74055.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bridge at <a href="/wiki/Gatwick_Airport" title="Gatwick Airport">Gatwick Airport</a>, under which planes can pass</div></div></div>
<p>Generally bridges are more aesthetically pleasing if they are simple in shape, the deck is thinner (in proportion to its span), the lines of the structure are continuous, and the shapes of the structural elements reflect the forces acting on them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> To create a beautiful image, some bridges are built much taller than necessary. This type, often found in east-Asian style gardens, is called a <a href="/wiki/Moon_bridge" title="Moon bridge">Moon bridge</a>, evoking a rising full moon. Other garden bridges may cross only a dry bed of stream-washed pebbles, intended only to convey an impression of a stream. Often in palaces, a bridge will be built over an artificial waterway as symbolic of a passage to an important place or state of mind. A set of five bridges cross a sinuous waterway in an important courtyard of the <a href="/wiki/Forbidden_City" title="Forbidden City">Forbidden City</a> in <a href="/wiki/Beijing" title="Beijing">Beijing</a>, <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>. The central bridge was reserved exclusively for the use of the Emperor and Empress, with their attendants.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Bridge_maintenance">Bridge maintenance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bridge&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Bridge maintenance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Example_HiFIT-treated_assembly.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2448" data-file-width="3264" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Example_HiFIT-treated_assembly.jpg/220px-Example_HiFIT-treated_assembly.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Example_HiFIT-treated_assembly.jpg/330px-Example_HiFIT-treated_assembly.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Example_HiFIT-treated_assembly.jpg/440px-Example_HiFIT-treated_assembly.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Example_HiFIT-treated_assembly.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Highway bridge treated with <a href="/wiki/High-frequency_impact_treatment" title="High-frequency impact treatment">high-frequency impact treatment</a></div></div></div>
<p>The estimated life of bridges varies between 25 and 80 years depending on location and material.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:12_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-:12-56">[56]</a></sup> However, bridges may age hundred years with proper maintenance and rehabilitation. Bridge maintenance consisting of a combination of structural health monitoring and testing. This is regulated in country-specific engineer standards and includes an ongoing monitoring every three to six months, a simple test or inspection every two to three years and a major inspection every six to ten years. In Europe, the cost of maintenance is considerable<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-34">[34]</a></sup> and is higher in some countries than spending on new bridges. The lifetime of welded steel bridges can be significantly extended by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/High_Frequency_Impact_Treatment" title="High Frequency Impact Treatment">aftertreatment of the weld transitions</a>. This results in a potential high benefit, using existing bridges far beyond the planned lifetime.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Bridge_traffic_loading">Bridge traffic loading</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bridge&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Bridge traffic loading">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>While the response of a bridge to the applied loading is well understood, the applied traffic loading itself is still the subject of research.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-engineer._2015_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-engineer._2015-57">[57]</a></sup> This is a statistical problem as loading is highly variable, particularly for road bridges. Load Effects in bridges (stresses, bending moments) are designed for using the principles of <a href="/wiki/Limit_state_design" title="Limit state design">Load and Resistance Factor Design</a>. Before factoring to allow for uncertainty, the load effect is generally considered to be the maximum characteristic value in a specified <a href="/wiki/Return_period" title="Return period">return period</a>. Notably, in Europe, it is the maximum value expected in 1000 years.
</p><p>Bridge standards generally include a load model, deemed to represent the characteristic maximum load to be expected in the return period. In the past, these load models were agreed by standard drafting committees of experts but today, this situation is changing. It is now possible to measure the components of bridge traffic load, to weigh trucks, using <a href="/wiki/Weigh_in_motion" title="Weigh in motion">weigh-in-motion</a> (WIM) technologies. With extensive WIM databases, it is possible to calculate the maximum expected load effect in the specified return period. This is an active area of research, addressing issues of opposing direction lanes,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> side-by-side (same direction) lanes,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> traffic growth,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> permit/non-permit vehicles<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> and long-span bridges (see below). Rather than repeat this complex process every time a bridge is to be designed, standards authorities specify simplified notional load models, notably HL-93,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> intended to give the same load effects as the characteristic maximum values. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eurocode" title="Eurocode">Eurocode</a> is an example of a standard for bridge traffic loading that was developed in this way.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Traffic_loading_on_long_span_bridges">Traffic loading on long span bridges</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bridge&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Traffic loading on long span bridges">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Forth_from_above.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3672" data-file-width="4896" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Forth_from_above.jpg/220px-Forth_from_above.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Forth_from_above.jpg/330px-Forth_from_above.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Forth_from_above.jpg/440px-Forth_from_above.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Forth_from_above.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Traffic on <a href="/wiki/Forth_Road_Bridge" title="Forth Road Bridge">Forth Road Bridge</a>, Scotland, before it was closed to general traffic. Traffic has now been moved to the <a href="/wiki/Queensferry_Crossing" title="Queensferry Crossing">Queensferry Crossing</a>, that can be seen on the left.</div></div></div>
<p>Most bridge standards are only applicable for short and medium spans<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> - for example, the Eurocode is only applicable for loaded lengths up to 200 m. Longer spans are dealt with on a case-by-case basis. It is generally accepted that the intensity of load reduces as span increases because the probability of many trucks being closely spaced and extremely heavy reduces as the number of trucks involved increases. It is also generally assumed that short spans are governed by a small number of trucks traveling at high speed, with an allowance for dynamics. Longer spans on the other hand, are governed by congested traffic and no allowance for dynamics is needed. Calculating the loading due to congested traffic remains a challenge as there is a paucity of data on inter-vehicle gaps, both within-lane and inter-lane, in congested conditions. <a href="/wiki/Weigh_in_motion" title="Weigh in motion">Weigh-in-Motion</a> (WIM) systems provide data on inter-vehicle gaps but only operate well in free flowing traffic conditions. Some authors have used cameras to measure gaps and vehicle lengths in jammed situations and have inferred weights from lengths using WIM data.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> Others have used <a href="/wiki/Microsimulation" title="Microsimulation">microsimulation</a> to generate typical clusters of vehicles on the bridge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Bridge_vibration">Bridge vibration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bridge&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Bridge vibration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Bridges vibrate under load and this contributes, to a greater or lesser extent, to the stresses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_35-2"><a href="#cite_note-:2-35">[35]</a></sup> Vibration and dynamics are generally more significant for slender structures such as pedestrian bridges and long-span road or rail bridges. One of the most famous examples is the <a href="/wiki/Tacoma_Narrows_Bridge" title="Tacoma Narrows Bridge">Tacoma Narrows Bridge</a> that collapsed shortly after being constructed due to excessive vibration. More recently, the <a href="/wiki/Millennium_Bridge,_London" title="Millennium Bridge, London">Millennium Bridge</a> in London vibrated excessively under pedestrian loading and was closed and retrofitted with a system of dampers. For smaller bridges, dynamics is not catastrophic but can contribute an added amplification to the stresses due to static effects. For example, the Eurocode for bridge loading specifies amplifications of between 10% and 70%, depending on the span, the number of traffic lanes and the type of stress (bending moment or shear force).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vehicle-bridge_dynamic_interaction">Vehicle-bridge dynamic interaction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bridge&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Vehicle-bridge dynamic interaction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>There have been many studies of the dynamic interaction between vehicles and bridges during vehicle crossing events. Fryba<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> did pioneering work on the interaction of a moving load and an Euler-Bernoulli beam. With increased computing power, vehicle-bridge interaction (VBI) models have become ever more sophisticated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> The concern is that one of the many natural frequencies associated with the vehicle will resonate with the bridge first natural frequency.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> The vehicle-related frequencies include body bounce and axle hop but there are also pseudo-frequencies associated with the vehicle's speed of crossing<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> and there are many frequencies associated with the surface profile.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-engineer._2015_57-1"><a href="#cite_note-engineer._2015-57">[57]</a></sup> Given the wide variety of heavy vehicles on road bridges, a statistical approach has been suggested, with VBI analyses carried out for many statically extreme loading events.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Bridge_failures">Bridge failures</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bridge&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Bridge failures">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_bridge_failures" title="List of bridge failures">List of bridge failures</a></div>
<p>The failure of bridges is of special concern for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Structural_engineers" title="Structural engineers">structural engineers</a> in trying to learn lessons vital to bridge design, construction and maintenance.
</p><p>The failure of bridges first assumed national interest in Britain during the <a href="/wiki/Victorian_era" title="Victorian era">Victorian era</a> when many new designs were being built, often using new materials, with some of them failing catastrophically.
</p><p>In the United States, the <a href="/wiki/National_Bridge_Inventory" title="National Bridge Inventory">National Bridge Inventory</a> tracks the structural evaluations of all bridges, including designations such as "structurally deficient" and "functionally obsolete".
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Bridge_health_monitoring">Bridge health monitoring</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bridge&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Bridge health monitoring">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>There are several methods used to monitor the condition of large structures like bridges. Many long-span bridges are now routinely monitored with a range of sensors. Many types of sensors are used, including strain transducers, <a href="/wiki/Accelerometer" title="Accelerometer">accelerometers</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> tiltmeters, and GPS. Accelerometers have the advantage that they are inertial, i.e., they do not require a reference point to measure from. This is often a problem for distance or deflection measurement, especially if the bridge is over water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup>
</p><p>An option for structural-integrity monitoring is "non-contact monitoring", which uses the <a href="/wiki/Doppler_effect" title="Doppler effect">Doppler effect</a> (Doppler shift). A <a href="/wiki/Laser" title="Laser">laser</a> beam from a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Laser_Doppler_Vibrometer" title="Laser Doppler Vibrometer">Laser Doppler Vibrometer</a> is directed at the point of interest, and the vibration amplitude and frequency are extracted from the Doppler shift of the laser beam frequency due to the motion of the surface.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> The advantage of this method is that the setup time for the equipment is faster and, unlike an accelerometer, this makes measurements possible on multiple structures in as short a time as possible. Additionally, this method can measure specific points on a bridge that might be difficult to access. However, vibrometers are relatively expensive and have the disadvantage that a reference point is needed to measure from.
</p><p>Snapshots in time of the external condition of a bridge can be recorded using <a href="/wiki/Lidar" title="Lidar">Lidar</a> to aid bridge inspection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-omer_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-omer-84">[84]</a></sup> This can provide measurement of the bridge geometry (to facilitate the building of a computer model) but the accuracy is generally insufficient to measure bridge deflections under load.
</p><p>While larger modern bridges are routinely monitored electronically, smaller bridges are generally inspected visually by trained inspectors. There is considerable research interest in the challenge of smaller bridges as they are often remote and do not have electrical power on site. Possible solutions are the installation of sensors on a specialist inspection vehicle and the use of its measurements as it drives over the bridge to infer information about the bridge condition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> These vehicles can be equipped with accelerometers, gyrometers, Laser Doppler Vibrometers<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> and some even have the capability to apply a resonant force to the road surface in order to dynamically excite the bridge at its resonant frequency.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Visual_index">Visual index</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bridge&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Visual index">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/List_of_bridge_types" title="List of bridge types">List of bridge types</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_longest_bridges_in_the_world" title="List of longest bridges in the world">List of longest bridges in the world</a></div>
